RATING: 8
The game involves buying and placing cards in front of you to repeatedly activate them gaining gold and victory points. The market changes frequently and cards become cheaper. It feels like a simpler streamlined version of Copycat. San Juan is another close relation. There is something charming about the odd child-like artwork, and the strategy is light enough to introduce to non-gamers.

I love this game and am excited about the upcoming reprint. This is the only reason I traded away my copy, in exchange for Chinatown.

RATING: 6

2016-01-21
5->6
After many years I have come to appreciate Saint Petersburg. Sure, it can be seen as a dryish game since there is very little of anything that is not polished and balanced. In other words, compared with many of the newer games that are overflown with twisty mechanics, bits and pieces, fluff and art, Saint Petersburg feels either ascetic or elegant. It is your choice which point of view you assume. Personally, I dislike the art and that cannot be helped but it does not break the game since it also serves its purpose.

Anyways, the game is nicely balanced and offers many tough decisions retaining tension until the very end. Set collection is simply fun and you have to keep an eye on other players as well. Interaction between players takes place at subtler levels of perception and there are virtually no "in your face" events. 

Despite being elegant, the game does not induce any greater needs to spend all your time with it. The game is mostly tactical with emphasis on short term decisions. When to buy and when to save money is the question. Moreover, Saint Petersburg can be very ruthless since there are no catch up mechanisms. I am fine with this but new players should be warned about it.

RATING: 6
Once upon a time there was a trout, living in a small river. It was a beautiful river, but rather shallow, and the trout was hungry to see the deep water. So it left the river for the big wide sea and swam with the big fishes. The big ones were very nice and friendly fellows, and they all played a while. But it turned out that big fishes like to swim fast, and the trout couldn't keep up, and things got awkward. So the trout stopped playing Saint Petersburg and returned to the river. The End.

(Really, Saint Petersburg is a wonderful game with great mechanics. But I made the mistake of repeatedly playing it with people who were significantly smarter than me. Turns out this takes away all the fun. Especially in a game that is about as random (and complex) as chess. Really great game. Just ... stay in your own waters, yes?)

This would have been an average game (just build the best/most useful of what you can afford), but because of the ingenuity of the 3-card hand coupled with the rules for drawing new cards, we have an interesting game.  (Imagine: this game would be pretty boring if there is no 3-card hand and a fixed number of new cards are drawn each phase.) However, some balance issues tend to make the optimal strategy monotonous, reducing re-play interest after many games.  A "8" or better with my variant.

RATING: 8
2-4(4)

Sometimes the gameplay seems a bit scripted and some of the decisions feel too apparent, but it's a tight game where the whole game dynamic is between setting up a big end game payoff and getting somewhat more marginal points each turn.

I like it ... it's a solid game system. The card rebates can make anything viable in any game. Buildings often get neglected early in the game, but if many markets come out, you can possibly get a batch of them for 15 rubles and now you're scoring points and primed for upgrades.

Issues I have: there's a brutal runaway leader issue ... not as far as points (though I can see it happening) but moreso with respect with cash. Getting an extra worker early on can really cascade into a big lead. Aristocrats can make up the shortfall, but are expensive and harder to get cost rebates and it feels like going with an early building strategy will peter out (heh) quickly. I don't really like the Observatory (I play with it, still), but it can make up the shortfall (or aggravate it).

It's dry and can feel pretty mechanical. It often feels like there are "right" things to do and while money is tight and the decisions are important, there doesn't always feel like you're agonizing over the decisions. Well, it'll take all my money, but I clearly have to get this aristocrat. Etc.

Lots of luck in the card draw, especially in the trading deck as far as which worker upgrade cards flip. You can diversify, but that's costly.

Aristocrat scoring can be pretty swingy. Obviously, you spend the whole game building your aristocrats, but it's a pretty huge chunk. It is what it is, I guess, but it makes buildings less interesting overall, I feel.

Still, I like it fine enough.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
A clever game using basic principles of economy and efficiency which require you to make decisions about spending (victory points) or investing (the money making).

However when all players understand that this game is about hunting the red cards, it turns into a game driven by luck. This is rarely the case and you should have played this game very often to understand this.

The free computer game can enhance your skill very quickly.

Some cards - like the Mistress of Ceremonies - are off balance and gives players a considerable advantage when played early in the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Saint Petersburg having players helping to build the city of Saint Petersburg. Rounds consist of four phases, with cards of that type being put up into play and players drafting cards until everybody passes, after which money and VPs are given for the cards of that phase's type in player's tableaux. Play until cards of one type run out, in which the round ends and bound VPs are given out. The player with the highest VP total wins.

Probably the quintessential euro-economic engine building game, even more so than Race for the Galaxy. The higher randomness and the player-driven phase structure of the rounds in Race means that its often better to accept some inefficiency just to leach off other players. Saint Petersburg seems to lack this same trade-off, since the round structure is fixed and in very round cards of every type will appear. Thus its better to go for the more efficient card possible. One the plus side, it's not too rules heavy (I think it's quite elegant for a game of this type) and isn't overly long. However, it strikes me as even more multi-player solitaire ('race'-like?) than Race for the Galaxy (although maybe I just not seeing how the interaction really work). I might want to get this, except I'm not sure of its balance and re-playability.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
[COLOR=#959595][SIZE=7]Spiel des Jahres Nominee 2004[/SIZE][/COLOR]
[b]Deutscher Spiele Preis 2004[/b]

[i]Posted: 4/9/06, Rated: 9[/i]

Point of reference: the first five times that I played this game were on [url=http://www.brettspielwelt.de/?nation=en]BSW[/url].  The  game flowed easily, and shared some characteristics with [i][GAMEID=8217][/i] -- another game that I adore.  The goal in [i]St. Petersburg[/i] is similar: namely, to score the most victory points.  Points are earned by purchasing cards, which in turn implies that money management is also a key consideration.  The trick is in understanding the fine balance between developing your money-earning potential versus investing in point-earning cards.  I loved this tension in [i][GAMEID=8217][/i] and its progenitor [i][GAMEID=3076],[/i] so it comes as no surprise that I dig it here as well.

Unlike [i][GAMEID=8217][/i], there are four different decks in [i]St. Petersburg[/i]: Worker, Building, Aristocrat, and Trade (Upgrades).  Cards from each deck are made available during separate phases of each round.  New cards are placed in one of eight positions, [i]if[/i] there is an opening.  Do you take a building card that you don't want, so that you have the possibility of acquiring a noble in the next phase?  I'm not sure if these decisions are part of "Card Drafting," since I can't find a concise definition of that game mechanic.  Whatever it is called, it works well.

When any one deck is exhausted during its particular [i]phase,[/i] then the game will end at  the end of the [i]round.[/i]  Therefore deck management is important as well, so you can bring the game to a close on your  terms.  I've seen a similar mechanic in [i][GAMEID=491][/i], and I really like it.

Finally, I was able to bring this game to the table.  And it turns out that it is a bit of an administrative quagmire.  What BSW makes so easy is actually quite a hassle.  Making change all of the time.  Trying to remember all of the cost-reducing effects.  [i]Keeping track of the number of remaining cards in each deck.[/i]  The paper money looks [i]fantastic[/i], but substituting poker chips makes the process more manageable.  These details become easier with practice, but they are still responsible for a deduction of a full point from my rating.

[i]St. Petersburg[/i] is a deceptively simple-looking game with surprising depth.  It has many mechanics that blend together very well.  The two, three, and four player games all have different flavors, but do a nice job of maintaining the same level of tension (unlike [i][GAMEID=8217][/i], which in my opinion is best played with only two or three players).  That's two games named after saints that are winners.  If a game entitled [i]Sao Paolo[/i] is ever created, hopefully it will live up to the standard.

RATING: 7
Wide options but a need to be flexible. Quick to teach and over in an hour. An awesome game. Works well as a 2 player.

Up'd to an 8. I like this game more each time I play.

OK times passes, years in fact. I have now played this over 100 times on line and predicatable, the experience isn't what it used to be. What is clearly apparent is that successful play is extremely formulaic. There is a clear rythmn to the game that needs to be followed. There are obvious choices and equally obvious actions to avoid. When playing the inexperienced their bad moves stick out vividly. However the big thumbs down is that the card draw, especially in the first and last rounds can decide a game absolutely. The luck of the draw is effectively a coin toss and I'm afraid it has diminished the game for me. 

I am obliged to reduce the rating once more. A game I will play and is an excellent progression for those moving on from Gateway standards, however once the mechanics become obvious, the enjoyment fades.

The woe continues. I am now actively bored by the game progression and increasingly infuriated by the turn of the cards when they aid one player and slap another in the face. There is far more randomness in this game than I ever realised in early plays. I have no real desire to ever play this again.

Up or trade/ sale with expansion.

RATING: 6.5
This has come out a few times now as a 3 and 4 player game, but mostly with the same pool of players. It gets rated very high by many players in our group and I only got to it very late, having to catch up whilst playing strong opposition. 

p: I must say my appreciation for this game has gone down rapidly, and never been all that high to start with. I am amazed that it gets the rave reviews it obviously does. What am I missing? Sure, it's lovely produced, has clear, nice and interesting mechanics, but for me it lacks in all those departments that make a pleasant game a classic, at least in my book.

Somehow I feel that between victory points, development points (money), varied abilities and upgrades, and even the option to manipulate card-draw, there should be a much more intricate game on offer than the one I got here. All these ingredients and too often the same bloody cake. You know it's gonna be a cherry on top, how to get it there, and once the cards have determined who got theirs first slightly above the others, you might as well stop the baking as you're just playing on to confirm the one on top.

The thing I miss most are dilemmas. Which card to pick, how to hinder, how to progress, and along which path? It's not a good sign when all players look at the same exposed cards and all know what "you should do". Even more so when they are right. If turn one is obvious, fair enough, but when the cards turned over in turn 4 still have clear homes then I'm getting worried. I am even wondering if all the "different game ingredients" are too similar in effect. Money is too much just another VP in the making: it doesn't really open the door to alternate strategies, it just tells you how fast you can buy your VPs. The leader can just about always buy their way out of the dilemmas thrown up by their fellow players, and also, "buy" dilemmas for people trying to catch up who cannot react to that, each turn making the gap bigger and bigger. Once behind, against reasonable competent play, there is no way back.

First play reminded me of the very different Kardinal & Koenig (Web of Power). Not because of the type of game, but because it has a similar amount of game ingedients and rules complexity. Yet it does it so much more with it. On all levels, but especially on how to use the 3(!) cards available, and on direct player interaction. And compare how Puerto Rico's building abilities affect and open up alternate strategies, and then look at this one. It's not badly done, but a classic? Enjoyable, and one I will certainly play again, but highly overrated.

RATING: N/A
I rated this with the expansion as a '6'.  

This game had some depth to it, but it was really really dry IMHO.  So while I want to play it to try to win one more time, I also would rather play something with some more obvious "Booo-yah!" elements to it.

Looking through my geekbuddy comments, I have to agree that this game is probably one of those games that gets better with repeated plays.  Which makes sense because it's from the same guys that did Stone Age.

...which brings me to my one SOLID gripe against St. Petersberg.  The Aristocrat scoring grid.  Now, keep in mind my first game I got SOAKED on this, so that could taint my opinion...  but it seemed overpowerful.  7 cards was 28 points, 10 cards is 55 points.  Three cards makes the difference of double the points....really?!  With the expansion, each additional aristocrat card beyond 10 is 10 points each.  In Stone Age (same guys, same phenomena, so the comparison is warranted), 7 cards is worth 49 points, but it caps at 8 cards for 64 points (which for 6 cards is only 36 points, but there's only a limit of 2 of each unique set anyway...)  Which explains why there's all these "alternate aristocrat scoring grids" out here on the geek.  If nothing else, stone age came later, and I can't help but think there's some mea culpa in the fact that it's nowhere near as easy to get even 6 unique civilization cards.

BUT, sore-loser griping aside, if you're all playing on the same playing field...  it's just something you need to know.  12 Aristocrat cards scored once means the worthiness of the cards is about 75/12 6.25 points a piece--essentially scored once at the end, while a 5 victory point building is worth about 20 points if it lasts 4 rounds.  Man.  I think I've been bitten by the St. Petersburg bug.

I don't think I'll be buying this anytime soon, but I have to play it again. ;)

----------

EDIT:  More thoughts.  The thing is, even if you're ontop of your game, even if you've done everything right...  it's still most likely going to become an aristocrat race.  Which, considering two dueling masters and turn order, and money, and everything... would almost make the decisions too plentiful to really make much of a difference in what you chose, which I'm never a huge fan of (yet I like decisions.  Such an odd paradox)

RATING: 7

RATING: 8
Nice little money/point engine thing going on. Liked it enough after the first play to want to play several more times.

Overall, a good game with tight money - not too long and several paths to victory.

However, spoiled a little (like Dominion) by the fact that it can become obvious part way through who is going to win. There is nothing worse than playing out half a game with the positions all decided.

Update: After several more plays and with more experienced players I am still really enjoying it and there is a lot more tension about who is going to come out on top.

RATING: 7.5
Es un juego bastante sencillo, de comprar cartas para ir evolucionando y conseguir dinero para comprar mejores cartas y puntos de victoria para ganar la partida. Tienes que tener en cuenta qué está comprando el resto de los jugadores..  y actuar en consecuencia. El juego tiene cuatro fases: artesanos (que permiten conseguir dinero, básicamente), edificios (que dan puntos de victoria), líderes (que pueden dar una porrada de puntos al final de la partida) y variado. Al final de cada fase (excepto la última), se aplican los efectos de las cartas (en cuanto a pasta y puntos de victoria).
Reglas sencillas, y bastante profundidad de juego. Es bastante matemático. Además, es un juego corto (1 hora aprox. jugando a cuatro jugadores).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Juego sencillo de cartas, pero con bastante estrategia. Se juega por rondas y cada ronda consta de cuatro fases que corresponden con otros cuatro mazos: artesanos, que principalmente dan dinero, edificios, que principalmente dan puntos de victoria, personajes, que dan un poco de todo,y actualizaciones, que mejoran las cartas anteriores. 
Tras cada fase, hay un recuento y se cobran los puntos/dinero que corresponda, excepto la fase de mejoras.
El juego finaliza cuando se acaba uno de los mazos.

Es un juego muy sencillo de explicar, pero exige bastantes calculos y unas buena gestión para asegurar la victoria. A mi es un juego que me encanta.

RATING: 6
At first I thought I would love this game, but it turned out to be rather monotone.

There is a runaway leader problem: An early advantage in income stacks up quickly and usually leads to victory. As an example, a first turn Mistress is very hard to beat. But it is not only the Mistress, it is at the heart of the game: if you get a better income early it sums up every turn. Because of that the game is not suited well for players of different skill levels, as the weaker player will just sit and watch how the other player is able to afford more cards to finally win by a huge margin.

Between players of similar skill the game can be tense, but it feels pretty much the same each time, as there aren't many different strategies to try and only very few cards with unique abilities (only the Observatory, the Warehouse and the Potemkin's village.)

RATING: 8.2

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Perhaps the quintessential economic snowball Euro.  Basically, you buy workers to earn income, you buy buildings to earn points, and you buy nobles to earn both.  Upgrades add variety and mix up the types (i.e. upgraded buildings earn money and points).  

The game boils down to "play a round, repeat until game is over."  I could see why others might think it too simple, or too repetitive, but for me, all of those are pluses - there is something very comforting and solid about this design.  The many timing issues and the constant need for economization beckon you toward strategic thinking, but the card draft always limits you just enough to stave off brain-burn.  The main challenge of the game is generating enough money in EVERY round to get what you want, and, overall, to ramp up so the switch from money production to VP production is as smooth and efficient as possible.  With two equal players, this one is very competitive.  Great stuff!  Too bad Tummelhofer doesn't do more straight-up designing - this and Stone Age are very solid mid-level Euros.  If you like Euros, get this one - now.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
What a weird game. Our first play was two-player, and from turn one I was the runaway leader. On every one of my turns I seemed to have advantage after advantage, and the advantages only snowballed after each round. During each scoring period, I acquired more and more money, until in my last few turns I had so much income that I started to get annoyed at having to count it all up. My opponent was also obviously annoyed that he had to sit there watching me count. 

By the last round I had nearly 150 ruples, eleven aristocrats, and more cards in front of me than I could count. I absolutely smoked the other player and I felt weirdly guilty for it afterwards. I don't know if this is just a problem with the two-player game or if I just got lucky, but seemed very odd to me. Not sure about playing this again...

RATING: 5
UPDATE: I have high hopes that the expansion might put this game right. After the time-wasting exercise that was Through the Ages and Scepter of Zavendor, I had a craving for a Saint Petersburg that was better-balanced. But until the Judge/Mistress of Ceremonies problem is fixed, this will remain unplayed.
---
This was fun for a number of games, but it ended up crashing very hard. The problem was just extreme imbalance in the cards on offer. A first-turn Judge or Mistress of Ceremonies virtually guarentees a win, if you can play minimally competantly for the rest of the game. Buildings are much too underpowered. As a consequence there are much too few ways to approach the game. These are mistakes Knizia would never have made.

In the end its sort of like a book or movie with a horribly contrived ending. You read it, and are enjoying it, but when you get to the end it's so bad, you can't even look back on the first half of the book with fondness. Such are my feelings for Saint Petersburg - a game that was clever and sucked me in for a while, but when I realized how unbalanced it was, it ended up being much more frustrating than if the game had simply been obviously unbalanced from the get-go.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
I was originally quite impressed by this board game masquerading as a card game. At it's most basic level, it's an economic game where one needs to establish engines early in the game to begin the growth cycle, which snowballs over time. 

Despite being pure chrome, the thematic touches on this game made me smile when they came up and probably bumped the rating up slightly, namely the Potempkin village which builds inexpensively and upgrade at a higher value, and the Czar (Peter) who functions as all 4 types of workers for the purpose of upgrading. 

However, this game eventually hit the dirt at 3, down from a high of 8 as I played it out. The game seems charming when you first sit down with it, but it reveals itself to be pretty basic, and very easy to 'mechanize' oneself to after repeated play. I don't claim to be able to play this optimally by any stretch, but I know enough about the game to not have a lot of difficult decisions to make in any given game, which is one of the main things I enjoy about gaming. The rating on this just kept dropping and it's now to the point where I am avoiding playing it on an actual table and haven't dusted off the CPU version in months either. I will warn anyone who has yet to play this on the CPU: If you enjoy it now think twice before hitting it on the PC, the play speed will ruin you on the table top version forever. (and incidentally: I feel the people trying to compare this to PR or San Juan are reaching a bit, at the most bare basic level, yes, they are both games which feature economic frameworks which you need to exploit to get victory points, and have some rule bending involved, but I don't think the parallels are anywhere nearly as tight as people make them out to be on here)

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
I initally rated St. Petersburg an 8 but after several plays it dropped to a 4 due to its mechanical, processional, and very limited gameplay.  Revisiting the game recently, I lowered the rating another point because I realized that St. Petersburg represents everything I _hate_ about European games- chiefly limited choices that deny the possibility of creative play, meaningless themes that have a tenuous-at-best connection to the mechanics, and lack of replayability engendered by repetitive strategies.  Some will argue that  St. Petersbug doesn't show any of these traits but for me it does.  I consider this to be one of the poorer European games on the market despite its consistently good ratings.  Nonetheless, it should be played a few times to get a feel for it since it is a functional and somewhat impressive design from a mechanical standpoint despite the fact that there is only one successful strategy that will provide consistent wins. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Personally, I enjoy more meat to my game, but I wouldn't usually turn this one down.  Playing this for more than 40 minutes is unacceptable.  It's not rocket science --- or even long division.  Wives, take notice.  :)

RATING: 7.6
Scheme: Card game where you buy buildings. The buildings provide money and/or victory points. Winner is who has more victory points.
The scheme is so simple that's almost frightening. It has been done so many times that's exhausted. You must try to exponentially increase your earnings turn after turn until you switch to invest in VP buildings. The game works due to the original mechanism for drafting and buying cards, which is the only original thing in Sankt Petersburg.

After some plays, I'm afraid this game has little replay value and you can play it without a brain. One all players have enough experience, the winner is determined by chance. However looks like I'm addicted :-)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Sint Petersburg was an early acquisition in my gaming 'career': my girlfriend and I played the game when we were at Spiel in 2004 for the first time, but waited until the Dutch edition hit the market. I can't remember much of our early attempts, but given my sentiments about this game, a lot of the 'theory' behind the strategies we pursued must have been faulty or even plain wrong. As a result, the game has been stuck in a dark corner of my gaming cabinet for a very long time. I could not sell it on as my girlfriend has special ties with the city upon which the game is based, so it kept on gathering dust.

Fast forward a few years, when an acquaintance of ours dug his copy ouf of [i]his[/i] cabinet, and invited us to play. I did so with some reluctance: snowball engine and vague memories didn't make me truly enthusiastic. But I persevered, and didn't do too bad in the end although I made some stupid errors. It felt a lot more natural to assess the path I had to take to get the engine up and running, and so could dispell a few myths which I had allowed to cloud my judgment. For one thing, there is no truly dominating strategy in the sense that you should always focus on one make of card. Do that and you'll probably lose. Sint Petersburg is much more about being flexible with what you're given as well as what you plan to do in the next few turns. Failing to recognise opportunity as well as some basic rules of hand and money management will result in loss. What [i]didn't[/i] change was the feeling that the human players are attempting to solve a sort of optimisation puzzle. The subject matter isn't carried over well into the main mechanics, let alone the game material. All that matters is the price per VP for every action you take. I won't say it is easy to transform your money engine into a point engine as the game progresses, and will say that there is plenty of opportunity to hone your skills here—but in your mind it remains a matter of rational economising and not of constructing a city in the middle of a mosquito-forsaken swamp.

My previous rating was a little too harsh. The engine is fairly clean and fairly straightforward and thus simple enough for me to grasp, even if many subtleties elude me for the time being. In the future I'll probably not be as adverse against playing this game as I was, but at the same time I'd rather play titles with a bit more story arc or drama in them.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Simple?  Maybe.  But that doesn't mean that it is boring.  As a player, you are always involved in the game--there is very little downtime.  There are subtle and indirect ways to interact with your opponents that keeps it from being multiplayer solitaire, yet you still get to build your own engine without disruption.  

There is that point in the game where you have to decide when to stop growing your economy (money) and start gaining prestige.  Figuring out when to do this is what makes the game so much fun.  

It plays fast, is appropriate for gamers of all skill-levels, and still draws in "hardcore" gamers who like something with a little meat on its bones.

RATING: 6.5
Fun card drafting game.  Seems very balanced and not too long for what it is.  Would certainly enjoy more plays of this.  

I don't like the endgame as well as I do the rest of the game. More often than not, the last turn can screw you, if the cards you need don't come up, and you're left with all the cash, that is basically pointless (worth only a few measly VPs). Perhaps there is a way to hold cards in your hand just in case, but the penalty if you don't play them is just too severe to do this. 

It would be nice to see some varying strategies too. In every game, players focus on mainly Workers (Green) and later Aristocrats (Orange), while building very few Buildings (Blue).

RATING: 7.6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Players are attempting to construct St Petersburg through the purchase of craftsmen, building and aristocrat cards.  Each card only scores (money, victory points) during it's "turn", so timing of purchases is crucial, as is managing the money.  Cards can be upgraded out of a special deck.  Nice purchase/drafting system, some special ability cards and discounting rules to keep you thinking.  Bonus points awarded at the end of the game for unique aristocrats.  Very brutal game on an early error (lock in your income first), and sometimes you can do nothing about it forcing you to watch someone run away with it for an hour. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I tried it once so far, really liked it, the components are gorgeous, although I'm not a huge fan of the paper money.  For the lightness of the game, the administrative tasks were a small burden at times (we sometimes forgot to pass the markers or rotate the card stacks), but I think this will be easier on the second play.  Kelsey enjoyed it too and we're looking forward to trying this again.

Update: I play this a lot on yucata, and I like it.  The only problem is that with players of unequal skill, there can be a run-away leader problem, which makes the end-game drag a bit.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Tanga
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Kipróbáltuk hárman ezt a kártyavezérlős, remek kis játékot. A játékban minden fordulóban 4 fázisban gyűjtögetjük (vásároljuk) a kártyákat, melyek magunk elé helyezésével építgetjük a várost. Gyakorlatilag vagy a bevételünket növelhetjük vagy győzelmi pontokat szerzünk, esetleg mindkettőt egyszerre. A pénzügyekre érdemes odafigyelni, mert gyakorlatilag csak két olyan fázis van, ami után tőkéhez jutunk. Mi gyakran jártunk úgy, hogy a következő forduló első fázisára nem nagyon maradt pénzünk vásárolni. A játékban kézműves, épület és nemes kártyákat szerezhetünk (az utóbbira érdemes odafigyelni, mert a végelszámolásnál különleges a szerepük). Ezen kívül vannak még a fejlesztő lapok, amiket érdemes begyűjteni, mert nagy előnyökhöz juttatják a tulajdonosukat. A játék 3 fővel kissé elnyújtott volt (két fővel még hosszabb lehet), de 4 fővel valószínűleg hamarabb véget ér a játék, hiszen a végjátékot valamelyik kártyapakli elfogyása indítja el. Az első benyomásom alapvetően pozitív, mert a szabályok nem túl bonyolultak, még a nagymama is képes volt vele játszani (a 7 csodával nem sikerült neki), remek családi játék (inkább felnőtteknek), egyszerre kell stratégiázni és taktikázni. A szerencse szerepe jelen van, hogy épp milyen kártyák bukkannak fel és ki milyen pozícióban van abban a pillanatban, ezt próbálták meg orvosolni azzal, hogy a kezdőjátékos jelzők fordulóról fordulóra vándorolnak a játékosok között. Egy negatívum volt amit majd a következő játékok alatt még megfigyelek. Aki az elején "szerencsésen" tudott vásárolni, az előnyre tett szert, amit végig nem sikerült utolérni, persze az is lehet, hogy csak én játszottam szarul :). Alapvetően egy ajánlott családi kategória, a játék során folyamatos a sikerélmény és a játékosok között minimális az interakció, maximum annyi, hogy ki jut hozzá másokat megelőzve a jobb lapokhoz. (2014.05.01.)

Kipróbáltuk négyen is. Rövidebb volt, de mivel most már négyen gondolkodtunk sokat :), így nem tűnt sokkal rövidebbnek. A játék újrajátszhatósága magas. Érdemes odafigyelni az első fordulóban. Nem szabad túlköltekezni. Ezt el is mondtam a többieknek, majd szépen túlköltekeztem, s hoppon maradtam a második forduló elején :). Összesen egy ember tartotta be a javaslatot, majd mivel mi többiek nem nagyon tudtunk mit csinálni a második forduló elején, ő szépen felvásárolta a kézművesek nagy részét és olyan pénzügyi fölénybe keveredett, ami döntőnek bizonyult. Tehát részben beigazolódott a gyanúm, hogy aki az elején előnyt szerez, azt nem nagyon lehet utolérni, másrészt az is beigazolódott, hogy bénán játszom :). A nyertes stratégia egyébként - hogy egy kicsit spoilerezzek - az volt, hogy az épület kártyákra nem nagyon koncentrált, inkább a kézművesekre és a nemesekre. Kíváncsi leszek a következő játékban mi lesz a nyerő stratégia. Egyébként még mindig remek játéknak tartom, de csak a felnőtteknek és AP (analízis-paralízis) gyanús. (2014.05.02.)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Competitive card game where players invest in cards for maximum return as the game progresses. Cards provide cash or VP during their active phase only, leading to an ebb and flow of income throughout the game. Plenty of room for advanced strategy between players, there are some nuances that can make or break a runaway lead if players aren't careful. The expansion cards are great and highly recommended.

30-45m, best with 2-3 players. 5+ plays.

RATING: 9
Had not heard of this game.  Someone mentioned it in Boston over a game of Brugges.  At least I waited a day before it made it to the collection, and another 2 days before I played it.
Seems that there's a point at which if one player takes a lead, it's hard to be caught.  It's a game that presents different opportunities each play.  SO whilst one generally strategy may work, the specifics will likely vary in response to the cards that become available in any one game.  That's the fun part:  having to quickly work through the options to optimize the scoring potential.

One game - got stuck with almost no access to aristocrats - despite mitigating actions like clearing space or using the Observatory - so there is a bit of luck in the card and player orders.

nb - need to check the expansion for base game errata

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
[2 plays]  First time I played this I rated it a 3.  It was a rotten experience.  All I remember is that the seating order and random events resulted in my not being able to get any of the important kind of cards (probably workers), and I spent most of the game feeling like I might as well not have been at the table.

Six years later I played again and had a fine time.  The game teacher gave us some good advice (buy two workers right away no matter what), and it wasn't the horrible exercise in futility that I remembered, but a fun and interesting game.  It was sort of a happy medium--it made enough sense right off the bat that I could function semicompetently, but throughout the game I also had further insights into the game dynamics and how I could do better next time.  All in all, glad I'm in a turn-taking game group that made me play again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Scales well from 2-4, recommended as a medium weight economic game for gamers.  Basically uses cards and money similar to San Juan, and is a game of economic management and logistics.  Decision making can be incredibly nerve racking, because it requires very careful and agonizing choices in money management: do you invest money in trying to increase your income, earn immediate points, or focus on long term end-of-game points (aristocrats)?  A very tight and tense game!  One weakness is that early mistakes in a 2 player game will cripple you by allowing a runaway leader.  The game usually lasts 6-7 rounds.

The expansion changes [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/350100]five cards[/url] for balance.  Recommended modifications are Czar/Carpenter (costs 3 instead of 8, and gives 2 rubles instead of 3), Observatory (costs 8 instead of 6), Mistress of Ceremonies (gives 3 rubles and 4VPs instead of 6 rubles and 3 VPs).  See also Tom Lehmann's comments in [thread=412907]this thread[/thread] explaining the changes.

Also owned: [gameid=29549]
Banquet is considered okay, but New Society is considered essential and must-have!  Randall Bart: "The New Society is a good, well designed expansion. Unfortunately, in the same box you get the Banquet expansion, which is a silly expansion from Spielbox magazine. Four of the Banquet cards break the game; they turn a well crafted play experience into a chaotic mess. It would be better to roll dice."

RATING: 2
Ugh, I really didn't like this game. The decisions started out fun and meaningful but dried up about half-way through. Then once everyone had their paths to winning, it came down to who got the good card pulls. I hate games that make you feel helpless, and I felt completely helpless in this game the way the cards fell to my opponent every time he was start player. He couldn't help but acknowledge his good fortune. [i]Oh look, you got a good card when you were start player but I got a crappy one when I was. You're good at this game![/i] 

That may be fine in a shorter game like Incan Gold, but this game is way way too long to have it determined by turn order that you can't influence and luck of the draw. Can't help but think of a variant where you can buy  start player tokens away from someone instead of buying a card such that if you are last and have nothing of real value to purchase, you could at least purchase the start player token for a future round instead of the tokens just going around the board.

RATING: 6
I've played this a few more times than my recorded plays suggest.

I see the early stages of engine building in this old game, and how it influenced others, but boy is this dry.

I like the mechanic where all your cards of a particular type give you their bonus in that phase, so there is no 'activating' a particular card.  Grabbing a card into your hand to prevent others from getting it is also good.

Other than that, I just felt like I was getting money to buy cards that game me victory points, and that was that.  My only interesting choice was whether or not to grab a card into my hand.

RATING: 10
Update:  A couple years later it's still a family favorite and my girls, now 13 and 14, still request it.  Enough said, it's a 10 for me.

  It's fun how quickly your economy builds up through a bunch of small, card buying decisions.  It's also great how the income and victory points are generated throughout the turns.  The game is always giving you something to work with.  It's light enough to be a family game but has and economic feel.  The artwork of the game really pleases me as well.  As your playing area becomes populated with cards you'll be focused on money but you'll also get to enjoy the games look and color scheme.  The card artwork is full of busy craftsmen, bug-eyed quirky aristocrats and fun drawings of historic buildings.  The artwork gives a whimsical personality and a feeling of life to an economy building game.  
     I prefer it with three rather than four, and use poker chips instead of paper money.  I'm not interested in it as a two player game.
   

Ok: I thought the poker chips were out of theme so I made a set of wooden coins with the paper money artwork on them.  Now it's perfect, it just needed a geek effort on the components.  It's and easy to learn economic game that doesn't take long enough to get boring.  My daughters 11 and 12 years old request it.

RATING: 4
Recommended by my Geekbuddies... (15 ratings, 7.92 average) - Comments based on BSW play: St. Pete is an investment game. Players start with some cash, which is used to buy cards that either produce cash or victory points. The cash that the stuff bought produces is used to buy more stuff. Repeat until the cards of one of the four decks of stuff is exhausted. The twist here is in the turn order, which can make or break you depending on what card appears when. If the turn order is against you, you'll be unable to buy anything good. The randomness in the game should be manageable, and players have some control over turn order, but with no way to predict what's coming up, the luck factor increases. It's an ok number-crunching middlewight game, nothing more. *** UPDATE: After more plays F2F, on BSW and with the freeware PC app, definitely a 6. The luck factor is very strong with 4P. I get frustrated with the card draws, and it doesn't help that the whole game is unmitigated card draws. A bit better with 3P, and best as a 2P game (which I'll never get to play F2F). Still, there's not a whole lot I can recommend here. At times, it St. Pete is just downright boring, sort of like a financial investment exercise where you have minimal control over what is available to invest in. F2F is a bit fiddly with all the money flying around the table every single turn. It's really not a bad game, and I'd play most of the time (especially on BSW or on the PC where I don't have to deal with the cash) but it's not a middleweight of choice for me. Removed from my wishlist. *** UPDATE: Now completely burned out on St. Pete. Nothing more to explore, and every play feels exactly the same as the last. The lack of depth is so glaring now, I can't even look at the cards anymore. I still can't believe this won the DSP. Dropped to 4.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
Saint Petersburg is an economic game. To improve, you'll need to learn when to buy and what to buy, and when to switch from upgrading your snowballing economy to buying victory points.

The experience is excruciating: you're always out of money, constantly trying to save money and invariably having to spend all your money to buy expensive cards.

Like this:
[b]A:[/b] What are you doing?
[b]B:[/b] I'm saving to buy...
[b]A:[/b] [i](interrupting)[/i] Gosh, don't you know piles of money in hand are dead money?
[b]B:[/b] But I want...
[b]A:[/b] [i](as above)[/i] You should be using it to buy more...
[b]B:[/b] Oh crap, now I'm broke! ...Again.

RATING: 8
St. Petersburg is a quite simple card game concerning the construction of the grand Russian city of St. Petersburg in 1703.  The Byzantine-like artwork gives this game its own unique charm.  Gameplay is quite interesting; you have to balance the collection of your workers (for cash), buildings (for VP), aristocrats (for cash, VP, and end game bonus), and traders (for improvements).  There are enough choices to be made on what cards to develop, so the luck factor is very small. I thoroughly enjoy this game!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
I've played quite a few games of this now (by my standards), and I can see the implications of the moves I can make, and understand how you can mess with the available spaces, and I really think it's a beautiful design - every move you make depends on what your opponents have done or what they can do. It's surprising that so much depth arises from rules that are so unrelated to their consequences.

Furthermore, I love the Russian theme - so much so that I've made some effort to find out about St Petersburg and Russian history.

RATING: 8
St Petersburg is a game that continues to entertain.  I understand the complaints, but they are easily resolved by placing specific cards (Mistress/Judge)in the bottom half of the card stacks.  I find that St Petersburg offers a fun filled, tense 45 minutes. Scales well from 2-4.

UPDATE:  St. Pete is a solid 8 for me, in which I doubt it will ever climb or drop.  I understand many of the complaints in which people claim this to be a system rather than a game with real decisions, but I have yet to over play it to the point that it has become over thought.  I believe moderation in number of plays, and time between each  allows for more longevity with this title, as it never goes through the aforementioned scrutiny.  Because of the points raised in my wondering thoughts St. Pete has remained a game (if the higher value aristocrats are placed in the lower half of the deck) that flurishes through intutive play. I think St. Pete scales well, but is best with 3, 2, and 4 respectively.

RATING: 8.5
[b]Saint Petesburg[/b] [i]Mihael Tummorhoffer 2004[/i]
Saint Petersburg is a well designed game, which a perfect relation between quality and difficulty, that can be played in less than one hour and scales correctly from two to five players. I often use this game as a "long filler" since can be explain in less than ten minutes but has a lot of room for tactics and strategics. As I dont own any pure fillers, Saint Petersbourg is a handy game to use in short sessions. The expansion for five players introduces new cards and it is a nice adition to a very good light-medium game, I own it but I think is not really necessary unless you want to play the game with five players. Fully recomendable specially for beginneers and casual gamers while it is still good and demanding for the gamer. It is really good with two or three players and when played fast is also a wonderful "long filler" as I said before. And remember, collect these green cards and make some money early in the game or you will be soon bankrupted...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3.5
While St Petersburg can be a close-fought, intriguing game, the random factor ruins it.  Yes, you balance managing your income and your points.  But nothing beat getting the "right" card when you are first to choose from that pile.  In theory his balances out across the whole game but in practise the small advantages add up.  Also, those who understand the intricacies of the game's mechanics will gain a distinct advantage when trying to put together a series of moves involving discounts, moves to hand, and increasing the overall value of their portfolio in terms of income, points and end of game points.  There is little variance in this game.  You must play it as efficiently as you can, occasionally interrupting the other players' strategy for bettering their economy or end game points.  When it comes down to it, you either buy a card, place it in your hand for later or strategically pass.  Normally the turn order determines who gets the better of each round.  And each turn's benefits are determined by the random draw of cards.  Not one on my must play list.

RATING: 8.5
Solid game with deep strategy. If you like depth in your games, St. Petersburg is a great choice. Some games seem to take forever to play, with progress creeping at a snail's pace; others seem to whiz by a bit too quickly. St. Petersburg strikes a nice balance. From the beginning you can see the pieces of a strategy coming together that you can follow to the end, and players immediately begin jockeying for position on the board as they amass victory points. It's a good game for a small gathering of friends who like to chat and socialize as they play.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A game of timing, staying flexible, and evaluating odds.  Once everyone has learned the flow of the game and is able to avoid the common traps, the outcome will sometimes come down to luck of the draw.  But in the meantime, interesting decisions present themselves, and there's much fun to be had.  I'm puzzled that St. Petersburg is often described as "light," since it requires detailed calculation, and since mastering the pace of the game requires some sophistication.  I'd say it's a fast-playing gamer's game with a fairly high luck quotient.  Playing the PC version repeatedly has ruined me for face-to-face play.  St. Petersburg is rotten with four players in my opinion; too little control and aristocrats are wildly dominant over buildings.  It plays well with two or with three.  The PC version is only good with three players, since the AI is not suited to playing head-to-head.  Even with three players, I win over 85% of the time, but at five minutes a game it was my timekiller of choice for many months.  Other than not playing with four players, my only suggested tweaks for the game are to disallow the Mistress of Ceremonies from appearing in the first round of play and to remove the useless Mariinskij-Theater from the upgrade deck.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Played 20+ 2,3 and 4 players. Plays in 80 mins, 60 ish for 2 players. I like the theme, employing workers to generate money, using money to buy buildings for prestige and taking on aristocrats who might generate a combination of money and prestige. When to buy which is the strategic element to the game and which of a given type to buy is the tactical part. It will not take many games to settle on a strategic plan but the short term choices are enough to keep the game interesting for a few more plays. New players never buy enough Nobles. To balance things I advise that you award less experienced players 5 or 10 rubels more starting money and make it compulsory to take 2 builders in the opening phase. There is no catch-up or rain back mechanism in the game so maintaining a balanced between different levels of experience needs this artificial intervention *UPDATE* After a dozen plays this game is proving to have more lasting appeal than I first presumed. *UPDATE* After 20 face to face games and 100+ games on the PC I feel that the game is solved. I play identically to equally experienced players so there seems to be complete agreement on the best way to play this results in a game among experienced players being rather dull and a game including inexperienced players makes me feel awkward because they stand absolutely no chance of winning. I have really enjoyed reaching this point with St P but I now feel I want to move on and spend the time playing something else. I'll leave it rated at a respectful 8. March 05

*UPDATE* Just played my 29th game and a new player won against experienced players. Having the expansion cards (not the purple ones) in the game might mix things up enough to throw out my view of formulaic play always winning. Back up to a "9". Sept 2014

RATING: 10
This is one of my yet another favorite games. Awesome for 2, tough with 3, even tougher with 4. Get your money, build your buildings, appease your aristocrats. The balance is very fine. One might think, that because the aristocrats bring points at the end of the game, that's the key to the game. Although certainly there's truth to that statement, I've seen people win with less aristocrats and with just the power of blue buildings. 

Don't underestimate the blue cards. They are powerfull, give you an early edge and if you get the right ones, will put you ahead very quickly, and make it harder for others to catch up. 

Very enjoyable game. Nice theme, even with pictury graphics. Conning and tense. Really worth getting and keeping it in your collection.

RATING: 5
I want to like this game, I really do.  The system is tight and it is fairly well balanced, and it uses a lot of mechanisms I like in other games.  

BUT

1.  No interaction -  bought it first, take that!
2.  No theme - could be rethemed as anything.
3.  No replayability - get gold, get VP.
4.  Kingmaker issues - situations where you need to take a card to prevent the person after you from taking it.  Sitting after the newbie is a huge boon.
5.  Really only one good strategy - get money, get people, score veeps.

All those complaints aside, its a solid economic engine game, and your first play will be a lot of fun.  A lot of the mechanics remind you of Through the Ages, but without the interaction, theme, and multiple strategies.  Its mostly a timing game.  The upgrades provide a little depth but overall there isn't much there.

RATING: 10
Fun game, easy for novices to learn, and has great replay value.I played this game several times in a row, and have some thoughts on strategy:- The Observatory building is a great card to get -- you might guarantee yourself victory if you get both of them.  It is especially useful early, when you can use it to get new workers while other players are struggling to get cash.  Later, it is better for getting upgrades -- building cards or aristocrats that produce lots of money are the best.  Only use it when you have a space left in your hand (or money and upgradeable cards).- Don't forget about the point value for variety of aristocrats!!  If you have 9 different aristocrats already, the next one is worth 10 more points, and may be your best play!  There are only 7 types in the aristocrat deck, and 3 are rather rare and expensive -- 2 are Mistress of Ceremonies, 2 are Judges, and 3 are Controllers.  So the key to getting variety is to get some of the 10 more in the upgrades deck.  The cheap upgrades are best, of course:  there are ones costing 6, 8, 8, and 10.  The Weapons Master costs 8 and brings in 4 rubles/turn, and the one for 10 brings in 5 rubles/turn -- these are critical and affordable ones to grab.- Cash management is critical!  You should try to get cards that will earn you rubles in rounds other than the worker rounds.  Earnings in the aristocrat round can easily equal earnings from the workers, and will allow you to buy more upgrades.  Upgraded buildings can bring in lots more cash, which can help you get more aristocrats.- Hand management is important.  Don't put big, expensive cards in your hand if you can't collect enough cash to play them in the next round or two.

Rules question:  are players allowed to look through the discard pile?  It's more of an issue when a player has the "Black Market" expansion card -- do they get the right to look through the pile at any time, or only at the time that they play the card?

RATING: 3
[Initial Impression: 3]
After initially reading about St. Petersburg, I was quite interested in it, but placed it in the "try before buying" column, and am glad I waited.  It does have certain features of a game I should like, and I even gave it a second chance after an uninspiring first play.  But, if there's a game that I would use to illustrate a "dry" game, it is this one.  It's a completely calculational exercise, with players simply determining future income and point values for each card as it becomes available, and that calculation is almost always the same for each player.  Those card values seem completely arbitrary and therefore uninvolving, uninteresting, and meaningless.  Pick any set of numbered cards, use that as the income or point value, assign costs, and you could easily play a similar game.  My second game was a little more interesting than the first (which I attribute mainly to the people I was playing with the 2nd time around), but it just isn't something I could ever look forward to playing again.

RATING: 7.25
Saint Petersburg is a nice card collection game, forcing players to carefully budget funds to make the best of the opportunities the decks offer them as the game progresses.

I can definitely see there's a lot more in this game than what I encountered on a 1st 4-player solo play, and that's only to the good- players need to control the cards coming out, as well as the income and VP coming in, while still collecting a good swath of noblemen to be successful here.

Because the income is limited to rounds where your cards produce, it's nice to have income every round, but at the same time it may be necessary to sit back and watch some rounds so the money is there when needed.

I'm starting this clever little Euro at a 7, but it has a lot of plusses and I expect with play it will go up from there.

RATING: 5
Got it for christmas - very keen to try this but I think I should thoroughly read the rules first. It seems one of those rules-sets where 'the youngest player gets an extra card' and 'the oldest player is administrator'

By that I take it it means one random player gets an extra card, everyone manages the money and pieces

Definitely looks like it's best with 4. We played with 3 and it took some getting used to, in the early part of the game the cards seem like they don't really do much but its the cumulative benefit... Next time I should buy more workers and trade them up asap. Not many traders were coming out though because players were passing in other rounds. This mechanic grew on me as the game progressed as it meant certain card types were scarcer.

Only other slight gripe with the game is that it doesn't really feel like much actually happens, it feels repetitive and the cards don't interact with each other much they just sit there accumulating points. Other games that last this long you can actually feel like you are doing something through the game and the decisions imposed on you can be quite hard; this had neither.

It's ok and I think it could grow on me but we'll see.

Played a second game of it and we realised we were doing one or two things wrong. This has to be one of the worst written rules sets of any game.

Nonetheless I now understand it a bit more and I think I quite like it. Balanced multiple paths to victory and lots of interaction between players and between cards.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
After one play, I like it! Fascinating card-driven game, where you are buying cards in each of four rounds per turn. Cards can get you income, victory points, income and VP, or upgrades, depending on where you are in the turn. One can use the limited income to buy more income-generating cards, or to buy VP-generating cards early on, but it is clear that the one who focuses on income-first will inevitably win the game, with a surge at the end. Simple mechanics, but somewhat convoluted enough to make it difficult to explain the first game. After that, it simply makes sense. The game flows nicely, although our first game took over 2 hours. With experience, this should fall to 60-75 minutes, tops, with ultimately reaching the published 45 minute mark. Lots of game for so short a timeframe. I'm looking forward to the next game.

Update (09/18/10) 3 plays: This game has been seeing a lot of gameplay lately. However, whomever can get that extra worker card/upgrade early on, be it from poor play by the player to the right, just by the luck of the draw, and/or table position. That 3 ruble delta is so critical that it seems to snowball into more income, and finally more aristocrats for the point surge at the end. The same goes for the other decks; there are some cards in them that are game-breaking, such as the observatory. Perhaps manipulating the number of slots along the upper row, in order to control how many new cards appear is critical. What to buy and what not to buy is also critical. Still, one cannot shake the frustration that some player position is always predestined to win.

Update (10/10/11): The more time passes, the more 'meh' I feel about this game. Yet another economic snowball exercise. Leave me out of it. Downgrading to a '7' from '8'.

Update (01/14/12): I am resonating with a number of users' comments who have rated this a '3' or a '4': Economic snowball, seating position critical, lack of meaningful choices (simply decide which card is better than the other), sheer luck in the draw of good cards. I have no compulsion to play this game anytime soon, and fellow geeks have articulated why. Downgrading to a '6'.

Update (05/31/12): Finally, traded it off to a friend as a farewell present. He will enjoy it more than I have. Most likely, will never play this ever again. Downgrading to a '3'. This game has had a very short half-life--nosediving from '8' to a '3' within 24 months.

Update (11/19/2014): Done. Haven't missed it in over two years. Downgrade to a "2".

RATING: 6
If you use 4 pair of 10-sided dice to track victory points, if you use the "phase marker cards" instead of the "wooden markers", and if you rewrite the rulebook in 2 dinA4 papers, you can throw away the original board, rules and wooden pawns. This way, the whole game (sleeved cards+money+rules+dice) will fit in a small metallic card coffin, for optimal storage and portability.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Hamar nagy kedvencemé vált. Szeretem a mechanikáját és szeretem azt is - amit egyébként sokan hibának rónak fel - hogy nincs benne sok konfliktus a játékosok között. Ennyi játék után persze kijön a hátránya a szerencsefaktor, hogy milyen kártyák jönnek fel, de ettől függetlenül nagyszerű játéknak tartom. 

A Sankt Petersburg társasjáték az orosz Péter cár idejébe kalauzolja a játékosokat. A játék kártya alapú és a mechanizmusa különböző kártyacsomagokra épül, minden körben másikat kell használni, így mindig új lapok kerülnek játékba. Ezek a különböző paklik a következők: munkások (kézművesek), épületek, nemesek és az úgynevezett cserekártyák (speciális lapok).
Kezdőkártyák segítségével kiválasztjuk, ki melyik kártyacsomagnál lesz kezdő, és az ennek megfelelő fa jelzőket szétosztjuk. Kezdéskor a részt vevő játékosok számától függően 4-8 munkáskártyát felfordítunk és elhelyezünk a két sor felső részén. Ezek után minden játékos kap 25 rubelt és kezdődhet a játék.

A játék több körből áll, amely körök 4 fázisra oszlanak. Ezekben a fázisokban mindegyik paklit egyszerre használjuk a következő sorrendben: először a munkások, majd az épületek, a nemesek és legvégül a cserekártyák. Annak ellenére, hogy különböző típusú kártyákat használunk, mindegyik fázist ugyanúgy kell lejátszani: először a játékosok akcióköre következik, amely fázis alatt az alábbi 4 akció közül kell választani: 

- Vásárolhat egy kártyát elveszi a tábláról és kifizeti érte a kártya bal felső sarkában jelölt árat. Utána elhelyezheti maga előtt az asztalon.
- Elvehet egy kártyát a tábláról és a kezébe veszi. A kézben tartható lapok száma maximum 3  de amit így vesz föl a játékos, az nem kerül semmibe. Így lehetősége lesz fölvenni egy kártyát úgy, hogy nem kell azonnal kifizetnie, csak később, ha ki akarja játszani.
- Kijátszhat egy kártyát a kezében lévők közül úgy, hogy maga elé rakja és kifizeti az árát. 
- Végül a játékos passzolhat, vagyis nem csinál semmit.

Egy játékos egyszerre csak egy akciót hajthat végre, de ennek a fázisnak nincs vége azzal, ha mindenki elvégezte a maga akcióját. Addig folytatódik, amíg mindenki passzol. Így annyi akció hajtható végre, amennyit a játékos akar (vagy amennyit a pénze enged).

Ha mindenki passzolt, pontozás következik. Attól függően, hogy abban a fázisban épp melyik kártyatípust használjuk (munkások, épületek vagy nemesek), a játékosok megnézik a maguk elé tett lapokat. Tehát a munkás fázis után csak a kézműveslapokat értékeljük ki, az épület fázis után csak az épületlapokat értékeljük, a nemes fázis után csak a nemeskártyákat. A cserekártyák fázisa után nincs értékelés. Mindegyiken vagy rubel van vagy győzelmi pont esetleg mindkettő. A megfelelő típusú kártyákért mindenki begyűjtheti a pénzét és hozzáadhatja pontjaihoz az jelzett győzelmi pontokat is. Minden fázis utolsó mozzanataként új kártyákat helyezünk a játéktáblára úgy, hogy végül megint 8 lap legyen elérhető. A felső sorban lévő kártyákat alulra toljuk és a felső sorba tesszük az újakat. 

A játékban a „szűk keresztmetszet” a pénz. A játékosok állandóan pénzszűkében vannak, így a legkedvezőbben, a legtöbb haszonnal kell elkölteni a rendelkezésre álló pénzt, vagyis legolcsóbban leépíteni a legjobb lapokat. 

Fontosak a játékban a cserelapok. Ezek lehetnek akár munkások, épületek vagy nemesek, különlegességük az, hogy a játékos előtt lévő ugyanolyan kártyákat upgradelik. Különösen fontosak a nemes cserekártyák. Ugyanis a játék végén még van egy értékelés. Mégpedig itt az kapja a legtöbb pontot, akinek a legtöbb fajta nemese van. Alapból ugyanis hét fajta nemes van, de ha beszerzünk azonos típusú nemesből többet, majd azt upgredeljük (cserekártyát teszünk a helyére), feltornázhatjuk a különböző típusú nemesek számát, akár tízre is, ami 55 plusz pontot hoz. 
A játékosoknak gondosan kell tervezniük, hogy a kártyák kijátszását a lehető legkedvezőbben hajtsák végre. Persze a jó tervekhez egyrészt jó kártyáknak kell feljönniük másrészt az kell, hogy az  ellenfél ne vegye fel a számunkra nélkülözhetetlen kártyalapot. 


A játék kivitelezése számomra hagy kívánni valót maga után. Egy tábla, pár játékfigura, a többi kártyákból áll, tehát a kártyák grafikája döntő. Márpedig a kártyákon lévő grafikák – különösen a nemes kártyákon – elég gyengére sikeredett. Persze tudom, hogy ezzel azt a grafikai stílust utánozták, ami akkori korban volt, tehát a hangulatért bevállalták, hogy a grafika ne legyen annyira szép.A játék magával ragadó, mechanizmusa jól működik.

RATING: 6
An enjoyable game, but hardly a very profound one. You simply buy things on each turn hoping to improve your position faster than other players at the table. It's important not to fall behind the worker/income growth curve in the first half of the game, which means that a player lucky enough to get an early Observatory is most likely on a fast path to victory. The more expensive buildings are a very specialised purchase as the bulk of the scoring comes from the end of game 'aristocrat' chart, but here again, you've got to be 'lucky' enough to obtain some identical nobles that you can upgrade to provide the necessary variety. In all a game that flatters to deceive, there's less going on here than first appears.

RATING: 9
One of the games that got me truly addicted to the hobby, Saint Petersburg is an extremely fun engine-building game. I love figuring out the point at which an engine that makes money should start focusing on scoring, a point that changes depending on the number of players and the way the cards come out. It's also an amazing two-player game where both players must carefully consider when to remove cards from the board in order to maximize the opportunity of new cards for one's self (and minimizing that opportunity for the opponent).

It's definitely a game that will be a permanent fixture in my collection - the only question I have is whether or not to upgrade to the reprinted version that I haven't played yet.

RATING: 7
I love Saint Petersburg and think of it as a light Euro. The cards are simple to understand, and because they are all out at once, you can easily explain them to new players. No need for people to puzzle out hands in secret. And I like that the money is money rather than spending other cards.

But there’s no catch-up mechanism, there’s no brakes on leaders. And the economics can be brutal – one game, my entire last round was passing to avoid taking the 5 point penalty for cards in my hand. And having to “pass” all the time is pretty annoying.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Two years or so ago I probably would have rated this game a 7 or possibly even an 8.  Played it quite a bit, found it relatively quick-playing and enjoyable.  Then the newness wore off, plus the very obvious problem of the Mistress on the flop -- player leading off the initial orange phase (assuming he/she held onto 18 rubles) had a huge, almost game-winning advantage.  I realize several fixes have been suggested, but you also have to deal with the judge on turn one and the mistress on turn 2 which are almost just as powerful as getting the mistress on turn one.  Right now, I rarely if ever play St. Pete, as I feel the game is seriously broken and there are much more inventive and creative games out there to occupy my interest.

2009 Update:  Have got in a couple of plays with the St. Pete expansion, which to some extent makes an effort to address some of the play balance concerns, mainly by:  (1) adding a really expensive green card; (2) upping the price of the Observatory from 6 to 8 and (3) adjusting the value but not the price of the Mistress.  However, the expansion doesn't adjust the price or value of the Judge, which is now the optimum first turn Orange buy (and almost as good as a first turn Mistress in the original game) but, what's worse, it introduces a new Blue card that allows you to "go fish" and get orange cards from those that have been upgraded, including cards just turned in by the player using the power.  In other words, for the same price as an Observatory, you get a card that is just about twice as good.  So, three steps forward, two steps back, meaning I have no great desire to play St. Pete at all these days.

2015 Update:   Still sticking by my "4" rating, but have played the newer 2d Edition expansion, which I feel improves the "scripted" play of the early turns by adding a fifth deck of cards (the yellow deck, tied to the market) and also a fifth player.  So, from now on, I expect all my St. Pete games will be with the newer version which I feel is far superior to the original.

RATING: 5
In a way there's less to this than I thought there would be, but it also has a 'distilled essence' quality about it that's admirable. This is mostly cash flow and timing. It's is a nice change of pace from heavier fair and features crisp pacing in a 45 minute playing time. "Gamer's filler," if you will. There also seems to be a fair amount of luck in the cards that flip up. Players can manipulate who has a shot at drawing cards and how many shots they have, but significant advantages and disadvantages can come of which cards are revealed. It's enough to dampen the competitive feel (without wholly undermining it). The two-player game is drawn out, tedious, and much less strategic (I give it a 4/10). Four is best, three is workable. Unfortunately, the four-player game under-emphasizes buildings (blue cards) and over-emphasizes nobles (orange cards). 

Update: first play in months. Just a hollow shell of a game. Seems really obsolete in the face of Dominion, and I'd rather play a lot of games over Dominion.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Economic snowball game reminiscent of Dominion. Gameplay consists of drafting cards and player interaction is restricted to taking a card so no one else can. There are two mostly separate curves, income and victory points, and your objective is to fit them together optimally within the constraints of random/limited card selection and game length. Time your slingshot better than anyone else at the table and you'll win. But is it fun? The computer version hasn't led me to think so, but then it doesn't exactly highlight the interaction aspect.

I must register a WTF about the special cards. Most of them just modify scoring. A few modify other rules, like the Warehouse and Czar Peter. Then there's the Pub which lets you take a special action to convert money into victory points: a little odd in context, but fits in well enough. Finally there's the Observatory, which allows you to tap it for a Magic-style library manipulation effect that sticks out like a surgically attached third arm. What is up with that? Did someone say at the last moment, "This game is way too dry, let's add [i]one[/i] card with a weird exploitable power"?
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
In person this game is BORING! Just slow and menotinous.

RATING: 9.5
At its best with two players, good with 3.  With four players you really need to add the expansion (which is a good idea anyway).

The typical Euro prides itself on having multiple paths to victory. This one really doesn't. There is pretty much only one path to victory; you can't neglect worker income or points from buildings or distinct aristocrats. You have to do everything, in just the right balance. And you will always be a dollar short.

Playing it well does feel a little like honing an algorithm in your head, and then practicing executing that algorithm as flawlessly as you can. That's not exactly a desirable quality in a game, I guess, but I find it to be a lot of fun, for a while. (Note: Dominion would feel just like this if you always used the same ten cards every time. St. Pete doesn't have a similar mechanism for varying the gameplay on each go.)

There's luck involved, but a lot more skill. There are many players on BSW who I just lose to time and time again.

>400 plays, >25 of these F2F

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Very pretty game, liked the gold ink in the cards.

All in all a Max/Min game that runs fairly quickly, but seeming cold and mechnical.

Not much player screwage other than being able to take a card off the table they wanted.  So it's all a big race on the score track.

Each round is played in 4 phases, the Worker, Buildings, Aristocrats, and Trading phases.
Generally speaking, workers produce money, buildings produce victory points, and aristocrats produce either.  Cards in the trading stack come from each of these 3 categories, except that when you play a Trading card, you have to remove the same type card (Worker, Building, Aristocrat) from play. The trading phase also serves the purpose of cleaning off cards that haven't been played on the board.

The real trick to me it seemed was to build lots and lots of workers, get this huge revenue coming in, and start buying large Victory Point producing buildings.

Seems like a one trick strategy to me, which is why I can't really rate it too highly.  There's no way to destroy an opponent's resources.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
A really nice interactive card mgt. game about balancing money & VPs (victory points) and plays briskly.  SP is a card game as boardgame that uses "Buying" and "Hand Mgt". as the central game mechanic and it's slightly reminiscent to Princes of the Renaissance w/o the added complexities or length.  Essentially, it is a game about CASH FLOW.  You want income early on but eventually you want to rollover that into maximizing the most VPs at the best opportunities during alternating turn orders.  Timing is almost everything so prepare yourself for it.  With four sets of cards, and thus, four different 'First Player' markers, SP ideally is perfect with four plyrs but it plays very good with three.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Juego de cartas de gestión económica que tiene su intríngulis en saber elegir el mejor momento en el que usar una carta para obtener la máxima ventaja sobre tu rival.
Juego de mecánica sencilla apto tanto para novatos como para jugones,de fácil montaje y aprendizaje,de rápido e intuitivo desarrollo, de relativa interacción entre jugadores (no hay un puteo excesivo) pero de feo diseño que no obstante augura bastantes partidas. Aún así,parece que subyace una línea general de actuación que tiende a repetirse en todas las partidas:

1.No hay que pensar a corto plazo en términos de fases, sino a medio plazo en términos de rondas completas.
2.Sin artesanos no hay rublos, ergo... compra tantos como puedas y al menor coste posible, ya que todos los artesanos sea cual sea su coste reportan 3 rublos. Aún así,para asegurarte posibles mejoras es recomendable tener una representación de todos los tipos, más de los baratos y alguno de los más caros.
3.Los edificios reportan PV. Conforme aumentan su precio la relación coste/pv es más baja. Recomiendo comprar de los más caros y únicamente los baratos (2-3) cuando convenga (no al principio de la partida) para asegurarte una posible mejora posterior.Al principio de la partida ir detrás en el marcador no supone demasiado obstáculo.
4.Los nobles dan rublos y pv. A más nobles distintos más PV extra al final de la partida; si se repite de alguno que sea para mejorarlos con posterioridad.Al principio de la partida recomiendo comprar nobles de coste medio-alto y dejar los más baratos conforme avance la partida. Incluso sería recomendable en la primera ronda del juego no comprar ningún edificio y reservar el dinero para los nobles.
5.Las cartas de mejora son las mejores del juego. Es necesario amasar bastante dinero con el resto de cartas para poder comprarlas, de ahí el tener buenos nobles y muchos artesanos.
6.Guarda cartas que te interesen que no puedas o no quieras comprar por el momento (a veces es recomendable no comprar una carta aunque se tenga el dinero a la espera de la actuación del rival o a la espera de qué cartas salen en las siguientes fases de la misma ronda o hasta en una ronda posterior); si además evitas que tu rival la tenga, aún mejor. Aquí está el puteo del juego.
7.Importante sacar el máximo provecho de una fase en la que tienes el peón de primer jugador; ser el primero en actuar no tiene precio.
8.Sacar el máximo provecho de las cartas especiales: observatorio, pub, tax man, etc ya que suponen un plus interesante a tu economía y permiten una mayor variabilidad en las opciones estratégicas.
9. Valorar si te conviene dejar más o menos huecos para colocar nuevas cartas en el tablero; pocos huecos te interesa si vas ganando y muchos si vas detrás en el marcador. Coger cartas en tu mano es una forma de salir de una sitaución poco ventajosa con pocos huecos libres.
10. Todo lo anterior queda supeditado al orden aleatorio en el que aparecen las cartas en el tablero de juego;en algunas ocasiones saldrán cartas caras al principio que no podrán comprarse y provocarán cierto inmovilismo; si las cartas más económicas salen al principio de la partida, ésta será más ágil y rápida. Hay que saber sacar el máximo provecho de ambas situaciones: en la primera guardando esas cartas que de momento no puedes comprar y en la segunda comprando tantos artesanos como sea posible. 

Nota 1: Falta un poco más de put.. interacción entre jugadores.
Nota 2: No parece que la escasez de dinero sea un problema con demasiada incidencia en el juego, lo que le quita un poco de interés. 
Nota 3: A priori no parece un juego de finales ajustados como el también comprado este mismo año "Samurai" o el "Alta tensión", juego también de gestión económica; parece más bien que el jugador que mejor sepa aprovechar sus recursos puede abrir una distancia a mitad de partida que puede ser definitiva e irrecuperable, lo que claramente le quita emoción al juego.
Nota 4:Hay una estrategia a aplicar durante el juego que si no vencedora al menos si muy eficiente; este hecho le da al juego un punto de repetitividad que no me gusta. 
Nota 5:Es cierto que sólo hay una estrategia para vencer, pero como todos los jugadores la utilizan se producen partidas emocionantes y ajustadas donde un pequeño fallo puede salir muy caro. Además la interacción sube enteros al ser un aspecto más crucial todavía. Lo que antes parecía un defecto se convierte ahora en una loable virtud.
